    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="dd30152313284f2c9a40ef81891113a8">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '2a79713a-6bb3-4fbf-b810-752f2c246241'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d232729967" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na (K) metal batteries (NMB and KMB) have gained tremendous attention as large-scale energy storage systems because of their high specific capacity, low working potential, and natural abundance. However, severe Na dendrite growth hinders the practical application of Na (K) anode. Here, a multifunctional interphase layer consisting of fast-ion conductive Na–Bi (K–Bi) alloy and sodiophilic (potassiophilic) Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (K<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>) phases, which i derived from a spontaneous reaction (denoted as BVO@Na or BVO@K), is proposed and prepared. The designed electrode exhibits enhanced ion transport kinetics and homogenous Na (K) deposition behavior, and finally achieving dendrite-free Na (K) growth. Accordingly, the BVO@Na displays a high Na plating/stripping reversibility of 960&nbsp;h at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, and the BVO@K electrode can be operated a cycle lifespan of 870&nbsp;h under 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 2.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> in symmetric cells. In BVO@Na||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full battery, it shows remarkable cycling stability (91% of capacity retention after 1900 cycles) and a high energy density of 281&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 16.2&nbsp;kW kg<sup>−1</sup>. The findings lend credence to the prospect of the multifunctional interface layer contributing to the design of a protective layer for alkali metal anodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202307628-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202307628-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Na (K) metal batteries (NMB and KMB) are regarded as a particularly interesting candidate for next-generation high energy density devices owing to their high theoretical capacity (1165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for Na; 687 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for K), a low electrode potential (−2.71&nbsp;V vs standard hydrogen electrode (SHE) for Na and −2.93&nbsp;V vs SHE for K), and availability of Na (K) source.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0001_R_d232729959e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0014_R_d232729959e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Acta Phys. Chim. Sin.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, based on Na (K) metal as anode, the high-energy density NMB (KMB) can be constructed, such as Na (K)–S, Na (K)–O<sub>2</sub> cells system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0015_R_d232729959e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0016_R_d232729959e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, uncontrolled dendrites growth of Na (K) and instable Na (K) electrode/electrolyte interface result from the electrochemical reaction between the high electrochemical activity of Na (K) metal and electrolyte, which seriously inhibits the development of NMB (KMB).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0017_R_d232729959e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Sci.">17</a>-<a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0020_R_d232729959e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Recently, much effort has been devoted to addressing these issues of Na (K) metal anode, such as constructing a 3D collector host to decrease the local current density and accommodate the vast volume expansion of Na (K) metal,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0021_R_d232729959e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a>-<a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0026_R_d232729959e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Storage Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> electrolyte manipulation to regulate the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> and enhance the interfacial charge transfer kinetics,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0027_R_d232729959e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem.">27</a>-<a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0030_R_d232729959e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and artificial solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) implantation to stabilize the electrode/electrolyte interface and induce uniform Na (K) deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0031_R_d232729959e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0033_R_d232729959e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, building an ideal SEI on the Na (K) anode surface has been demonstrated as one of the most effective strategies. Various artificial SEI layers have been constructed, including NaF, Na<sub>3</sub>P, and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi protective layers,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0007_R_d232729959e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0034_R_d232729959e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Eng. J.">34</a>-<a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0040_R_d232729959e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Energy Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to regulate Na (K) deposition behavior. For example, Ma and co-workers reported a NaF-rich SEI layer with high Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity, but the modified Na electrode only exhibited 300 h at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> in a Na symmetric cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0033_R_d232729959e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, Sun and co-workers proposed to improve the cyclic stability of Na anode by introducing a high sodiophilic Na–Bi alloy interfacial layer, while the assembled symmetric cells could only be operated at the limited time (≤500&nbsp;h) at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0041_R_d232729959e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, a long cycle life (&gt;800&nbsp;h) of sodium metal anode at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> by constructing only a single principal component protective layer is difficult to realize. It attributes to a single functional design which only focuses on a single aspect of artificial protective layer requirements, such as high Na<sup>+</sup> (K<sup>+</sup>) conductivity or strong mechanical toughness, and without considering its overall characteristics. Therefore, it is much urgent to construct a multifunctional heterogenous interphase layer on Na (K) anode surface that gather with high sodiophilic properties, strong mechanical strength, improved ionic conductivity, and electronic insulation for inducing uniform Na (K) deposition and dendrite-free growth.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0004_R_d232729959e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0042_R_d232729959e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose a multifunctional heterogenous interphase layer composed of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (K<sub>3</sub>Bi) alloy nanoparticles and Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (K<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>) phases (BVO@Na; BVO@K) via a spontaneous electrochemical reaction. This particular multifunctional protective layer possesses the following merits. i) Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (K<sub>3</sub>Bi) presents favorable Na (K) affinity and a low Na<sup>+</sup> (K<sup>+</sup>) diffusion barrier, which facilitates rapid diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> across the artificial interphase layer. ii) Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (K<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>) presents good electronic insulation, which can ensure Na<sup>+</sup> only capture electron at BVO interphase layer/bare Na interface, achieving the regulation of ion transport and the Na homogeneous deposition. iii) The heterogenous interphase layer displays the muscular mechanical toughness, which effectively inhibits Na (K) dendrites growth. Benefiting from this multifunctional synergistic effect, an ultralong cycle life (960&nbsp;h for NMB; 870&nbsp;h for KMB) and highly reversible Na (K) plating/stripping at high current density and areal capacity (6.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> for NMA; 8.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> for KMA) are obtained for both BVO@Na||BVO@Na and BVO@K||BVO@K, respectively. By coupling with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) electrode, the full cell displays cyclic stability (91% capacity retention for 1900 cycles) and superior rate performance (91 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>). As for BVO@K||PTCDA full cells, it also achieves a unique rate capability and elongated lifespan. This work may provide a new method to design multifunctional interphase layers for high-performance of alkali metal batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202307628-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202307628-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The multifunctional artificial interphase layer was fabricated via rolling BiVO<sub>4</sub> powder (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) directly onto the Na metal surface (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0001">1a</a>). After an in situ electrochemical reaction between the metallic Na and BiVO<sub>4</sub>, the surface of Na metal foil changed from silvery to black (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), and then an artificial protective layer consisting of Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (abbreviated as BVO@Na) on the surface of the Na foil was formed, which integrates favorable sodiophilic, superior Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity, and electronic insulation properties. Benefiting from these merits, Na can be homogeneously deposited beneath the multifunctional interphase layer. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0001">1b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of BVO@Na confirms the generation of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phase (JCPDS card No. 74-1161) and Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> phase (JCPDS card No. 00-032-1199) in the artificial interphase layer. Furthermore, no diffraction peaks of BiVO<sub>4</sub> are detected, indicating the alloying reaction between BiVO<sub>4</sub> and the Na foil can only produce Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0001">1c,d</a> shows the phase structure of the artificial protective layer. Obviously, three clear lattice spacing of 0.272, 0.215, and 0.230&nbsp;nm can be observed, which correspond to the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (110), Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (104), and Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (311) planes, respectively. Element species of the BVO were verified by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0001">1e</a>, the two prominent peaks at 516.2&nbsp;and 523.5&nbsp;eV can index the 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbital peaks of the V<sup>5+</sup> state, respectively, which is consistent with the above the XRD mentioned above results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0043_R_d232729959e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A dense and smooth morphology of the BVO@Na electrode is found, and the thickness of BVO is ≈7.5&nbsp;µm in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0001">1f,g</a>. The coexistence of signals of Bi and V elements above Na foils is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0001">1g</a>, indicating that Bi and V elements are uniformly distributed on the protective layer surface. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0001">1h–j</a>, the BVO protective layer exhibits a high Young's modulus value of 8.9 GPa than that of bare Na (2.3 GPa), demonstrating that the BVO protective layer possesses enough capability to inhibit the growth of Na dendrite and maintain the interface mechanical stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307628-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9f4886ec-cfee-42f0-a1ec-5e6dc38cd8c4/adfm202307628-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9f4886ec-cfee-42f0-a1ec-5e6dc38cd8c4/adfm202307628-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/24911404-f877-4c5e-99e7-4b9d8d20fe7c/adfm202307628-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9f4886ec-cfee-42f0-a1ec-5e6dc38cd8c4/adfm202307628-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307628-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of preparation of BVO@Na protection layer on the Na metal. b) XRD pattern, and c,d) HRTEM image of BVO@Na. e) XPS spectra of V 2p for symmetrical BVO@Na cell before cycle. f) Surface and g) section morphology and the corresponding elemental distribution mapping of BVO@Na. h,i) The distribution of Young's modulus on the surface of BVO@Na and Bare Na, respectively, and j) the corresponding value of Young's modulus.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate the effectiveness of the protective layer, the cyclic stability of symmetrical cells were studied in a carbonate-based electrolyte. In <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0002">2a</a>, the BVO@Na||BVO@Na shows a long cycle lifespan of 960&nbsp;h with a low voltage hysteresis of 0.09&nbsp;V at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. By contrast, bare Na||bare Na only displays a short cycle life of 130&nbsp;h with a large overpotential of 0.12&nbsp;V, which is caused by the unstable bare Na/electrolyte interface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The different thicknesses of BVO layer (BVO-6.0, BVO-7.5, BVO-12.5) were successfully prepared (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a> (Supporting Information) shows the cycling performance of the different thickness of BVO layer for Na electrodes at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Obviously, BVO-7.5@Na electrode shows the most stable cyclic stability and lowest polarization voltage among other samples, demonstrating the optimizing the thickness of the BVO layer is an effective measure to enhance the cyclic stability of the Na electrode. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the Bi@Na symmetric cell exhibits flat voltage plateaus for 228&nbsp;h in comparison with the bare Na electrode (130&nbsp;h), indicating that the Bi–Na alloy protective layer can enhance the cycle performance but the improvement effect was limited. The outstanding cycling stability of the BVO@Na||BVO@Na is far superior to most previously reported results for Na metal anode electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0002">2b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0044_R_d232729959e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano Energy.">44</a>-<a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0051_R_d232729959e739" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nano Lett.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even at 2.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a capacity of 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the BVO@Na||BVO@Na can still stable operate over 100&nbsp;h, and while the bare Na||Na start to fluctuate soon and subsequent the cell failure (only 14&nbsp;h) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The value <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> of bare Na||Na varies greatly from 600 Ω at 5th cycle to 490 Ω at 50th cycle (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). By contrast, the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> of BVO@Na||BVO@Na symmetric cell remains nearly constant, from 250 Ω at 5th cycle to 216 Ω at 50th cycle, suggesting the good interfacial stability of BVO@Na. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0002">2c</a>, the BVO@Na||BVO@Na displays much lower voltage hysteresis than that of the bare Na||bare Na at all current density test states, indicating the BVO can effectively reduce the polarization to enhance the Na transport kinetics. Two plateaus (marked as plateaus “a” and “b”) are observed at the deposition curve of bare Na||Na in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0002">2d</a>, which represent Na exfoliation from Na dendrites and fresh unused Na, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0052_R_d232729959e767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Angew. Chem.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the stripping process, the fraction of “a” platform is close to 50% for bare Na, indicating the severe growth of Na dendrites and leaving much dead Na on the bare Na electrode. For comparison, there is a much smaller fluctuation for BVO@Na than that of bare Na, indicating the protective layer can effectively suppress the dendritic Na growth. The Na plating behavior of both electrodes was visualized by using in situ optical microscopy. Mossy-like Na appears on the bare Na electrode after deposition for 30&nbsp;min and subsequently grows (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0002">2e</a>). On the contrary, BVO@Na presents a relatively smooth texture throughout the deposition process, confirming the protective layer plays a vital role in stabilizing the interface stability and inhibiting dendritic Na growth. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0002">2f</a>, a smooth, dense and dendrite-free morphology of deposited BVO@Na electrode is observed, which further confirms the BVO suppresses the formation of Na dendrites. In sharp contrast, a rough, porous and loose surface of bare Na with many cracks under the same condition is displayed, which is caused by uncontrollable dendrite growth of the Na anode and severe side reactions between the active Na and electrolyte. Even after 100 cycles, a smooth and dense surface of BVO@Na electrode without the formation of Na dendrites was observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), demonstrating that the multifunctional interphase layer can regulate the Na deposition behavior and induce dendrite-free growth of Na.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307628-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4194429e-6518-4edf-9fd9-ef39b28e945c/adfm202307628-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4194429e-6518-4edf-9fd9-ef39b28e945c/adfm202307628-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/71a4fe3a-dba0-4a55-9b04-6469764ce610/adfm202307628-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4194429e-6518-4edf-9fd9-ef39b28e945c/adfm202307628-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307628-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performance of symmetrical bare Na, BVO@Na cells at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) A comparison of the cycle stability of our BVO@Na with state-of-the-art sodium–metal anodes. c) Rate performance of symmetrical bare Na, BVO@Na cells at 1.0–6.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. d) Plating/stripping profiles at 2.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> of symmetrical bare Na, BVO@Na cell. e) In situ optical microscopy observation of the Na plating process for bare Na and BVO@Na (scale bar: 200&nbsp;µm). f) SEM of the 5th cycled for bare Na and BVO@Na electrode.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition, the intensity of redox peaks for BVO@Na is much higher (almost one point five times) as well as the larger loop curve area compared with bare Na, confirming the faster kinetics in BVO@Na (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0003">3a</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0053_R_d232729959e819" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reversible behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> was elaborated by CV tests for first three cycles in Na||Cu and BVO@Na||Cu cells. Obviously, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) verified the good reversibility of Na<sup>+</sup> in BVO@Na in comparison with bare Na during the plating/stripping process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0054_R_d232729959e832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Angew. Chem.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0003">3b</a>, the exchange current density increases from 0.016&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> of bare Na to 0.501&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> of BVO@Na||BVO@Na, further verifying the faster reaction kinetics in the BVO@Na. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy measurement was performed to assess the activation energy of Na ions diffusion across the artificial protective layer. The Nyquist plots of both bare Na||bare Na and BVO@Na||BVO@Na at various temperatures from 40 to 60 °C are shown (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0003">3c,d</a>). The impedance of SEI (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) for bare Na||bare Na is 120, 89, 62, 58, and 45 Ω, respectively. By introducing the BVO protective layer, all of the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> values for BVO@Na||BVO@Na decrease significantly at the same tests condition. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0003">3e</a>, the value of activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) for BVO@Na (40.99 KJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is much lower than that of bare Na (52.13 KJ mol<sup>−1</sup>), indicating the faster Na<sup>+</sup> transport in BVO@Na. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0003">3f</a>, the nucleation overpotential of 0.06&nbsp;V is obtained for the bare Na. In the case of BVO@Na, it displays a lower nucleation overpotential of 0.01&nbsp;V, demonstrating that the nucleation of Na is favorable on BVO protective layer. These results further demonstrate that the BVO layer can enhance Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics and realize even Na deposition.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307628-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d49d11f4-c7f3-41b5-b335-25bec5ae0c35/adfm202307628-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d49d11f4-c7f3-41b5-b335-25bec5ae0c35/adfm202307628-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7de9e892-2525-498c-8c2d-586aa70d144c/adfm202307628-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d49d11f4-c7f3-41b5-b335-25bec5ae0c35/adfm202307628-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307628-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cyclic voltammetry using Na||Cu cells of bare Na and BVO@Na. b) Tafel profile obtained from cyclic voltammetry measurements. c) Bare Na and d) BVO@Na cells at different temperatures. The inset is the equivalent circuit model. e) The activation energy for Na ions diffusion through SEI. f) The nucleation overpotential of bare Na and BVO@Na at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further understand the role played by a multifunctional protective layer in the Na deposition process, the first-principles calculations were studied (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0004">4</a>). The calculated adsorption energies of Na on Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (001) and Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (110) are −1.17 and −4.94&nbsp;eV, respectively, while it is −0.80&nbsp;eV for bare Na (001), indicating the outstanding sodiophilicity of BVO protective layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0004">4a–c</a>). When applied as a protective layer, it contributes to reducing the nucleation overpotential and promotes even Na deposition on the Na anode-modified. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0004">4e,f</a>, the Na ions can easily migrate across the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (110) surface with a much lower energy barrier of 0.04&nbsp;eV, while the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> along the Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> interface is difficult due to its higher energy barrier (0.90&nbsp;eV). Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> acts as an electronic insulator for the protective layer with a large bandgap of 3.67&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0004">4g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), which can ensure Na<sup>+</sup> only capture electron at BVO interphase layer/bare Na interface during the process of passing across the BVO protective layer, and allows for even deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> on the protective layer/bare Na interface. The interface energy (γ) values of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi/Na and Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>/Na are −0.05 and −0.11&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−2</sup>, respectively, demonstrating the improved Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics along the Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>/Na interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0004">4h–j</a>). According to the Griffith theory, the dendrite suppression ability and stability of the protective layer can be evaluated by the product of the γ and Young's modulus (<i>E</i>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0055_R_d232729959e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Nano.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information) shows the high γ<i>E</i> value of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi is 1585 meV Å<sup>−2</sup> GPa<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that the protective layer possesses strong enough mechanical toughness to maintain the interface stability and induce uniform deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> during long cycle life. As a result, the BVO multifunctional protective layer can function as a robust artificial SEI layer with fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and electronic insulation characteristics, as well as a strong dendrite inhibition ability, leading to the realization of dendrite-free Na growth and outstanding cyclic stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0004">4k</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307628-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/46d500a4-7a6e-4939-9fa6-e85cb9e9ec62/adfm202307628-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/46d500a4-7a6e-4939-9fa6-e85cb9e9ec62/adfm202307628-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/125bf542-25fd-4355-a8df-9a0c1e906b87/adfm202307628-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/46d500a4-7a6e-4939-9fa6-e85cb9e9ec62/adfm202307628-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307628-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The geometry-optimized atomic configuration of Na atoms absorbed on a) Na (001), b) Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (110), c) Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (010), respectively, and d) corresponding adsorption energy. Diffusion path of Na<sup>+</sup> on e) Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (110), f) Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (010) surfaces, respectively. g) Band structure of Na3VO4 interphase. h,i) Atomic structures of (h) Na3Bi (001)/Na (001) and (i) Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (001)/Na (001) interfaces. j) The values of the surface energy (<i>γ</i>) for Na<sub>3</sub>Bi and Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>. k) Schematic illustration of the multifunctional protective layer.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate the practicability of the BVO@Na anode, the full battery comprising Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode and BVO@Na anode (BVO@Na||NVP) was assembled (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0005">5b</a> displays the considerable rate capabilities of 108, 107, 105, 103, 100, 97, and 91 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for BVO@Na||NVP at the current rates of 0.1–5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current turns back to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, BVO@Na||NVP still maintains a specific capacity of 107 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. A much smaller polarization voltage of BVO@Na||NVP compared with those of bare Na||NVP was observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0005">5c,d</a>). For comparison, the bare Na||NVP only displays a low capacity of 42 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0005">5e</a>, BVO@Na||NVP displays a high energy density of 281&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 16.2&nbsp;kW kg<sup>−1</sup>, which outperforms most of the previously reported results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0039_R_d232729959e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Environ. Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0056_R_d232729959e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Energy Mater.">56</a>-<a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0062_R_d232729959e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Small.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the value of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for BVO@Na||NVP almost remains unchanged with the cycling (from 50 to 300 cycles), indicating the good interfacial stability of BVO@Na||NVP during the long-term cycle life. To further verify its electrochemical performance in practical application of high-voltage sodium metal batteries, the full cells were assembled with BVO@Na anodes and high-voltage Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub> (NVPF) cathodes, and the BVO@Na||NVPF full cell exhibits enhanced rate capability in comparison to bare Na||NVPF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a</a>, Supporting Information). BVO@Na||NVPF can deliver high rate capacities of 123, 122, 121, 117, 110, 91, 79, 68, and 59 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at various current rates of 0.05–5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The energy density of BVO@Na||NVPF is 458&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> with a high working voltage of 3.67&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a>, Supporting Information). Even at 193&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, BVO@Na||NVPF still achieves an energy density of 16.2&nbsp;kW Kg<sup>−1</sup>. Their galvanostatic charge/discharge curves from 0.05 to 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). All polarization voltage values of BVO@Na||NVPF are much smaller than those of bare Na||NVPF at 0.05–1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> transport dynamics. To further evaluate the merits of the BVO, the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of BVO@Na||NVP was measured in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0005">5f</a>. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of bare Na||NVP decreases from 200 to 30 Ω for BVO@Na||NVP, indicating the faster Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics with the help of the BVO. The long cycling stability of both Na||NVP and BVO@Na||NVP is evaluated at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0005">5g</a>. The BVO@Na||NVP achieves a high capacity of 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and it still maintains a specific capacity of 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high capacity retention of 91% after 1900 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, while the bare Na||NVP delivers a severe capacity decay after 600 cycles.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307628-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6cc8d797-c496-4704-8a18-1e13d1dc3d79/adfm202307628-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6cc8d797-c496-4704-8a18-1e13d1dc3d79/adfm202307628-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c2dbafc6-f905-468f-8af8-7492472e5d0c/adfm202307628-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6cc8d797-c496-4704-8a18-1e13d1dc3d79/adfm202307628-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307628-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the BVO@Na||NVP full cell. b) Rate performance for bare Na||NVP and BVO@Na||NVP. Galvanostatic charge−discharge profiles for c) BVO@Na||NVP and d) bare Na||NVP obtained at different currents. e) The Ragone plots showing energy and power densities versus other references. f) Nyquist plots of the bare Na||NVP and BVO@Na||NVP measured after 5 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Cycling stability at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> for bare Na||NVP and BVO@Na||NVP.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further demonstrate the superiority of our designed artificial protective layer, the plating/stripping reversibility of the BVO@K electrode was investigated. After rolling BiVO<sub>4</sub> powders on the K surface, the silvery surface of K metal changed to black (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information), and subsequently an artificial protective layer on the K foil surface (BVO@K) was formed. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a–c</a> (Supporting Information), the XRD pattern of BVO@K confirms the generation of K<sub>3</sub>Bi phase (JCPDS No. 74-1165) and K<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> phase (JCPDS No. 00-029-1065) in the artificial interphase layer. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21d</a> (Supporting Information), the two prominent peaks at 515.8&nbsp;and 523.4&nbsp;eV can be indexed to the 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbital peaks of V<sup>5+</sup> state, respectively, which is consistent with the XRD results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0043_R_d232729959e1216" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21e</a> (Supporting Information) presents a uniform distribution of V and Bi elements on BVO protective layer. A dense and smooth morphology of BVO@K electrode was found, and the thickness of BVO was ≈10&nbsp;µm in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21e,f</a> (Supporting Information). The nucleation overpotential decreases from 0.71&nbsp;V for bare K to 0.07&nbsp;V for BVO@K (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). A stable voltage profile of BVO@K symmetric cell (BVO@K||BVO@K) with a small hysteresis over 530&nbsp;h, indicates enhanced K transport kinetics (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0006">6a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). The bare K||bare K only delivers a short cycle life of 90&nbsp;h, which is attributed to the uncontrolled dendritic K growth. Even at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 2.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the BVO@K||BVO@K achieves a remarkable long cycle life over 870&nbsp;h with a low overpotential of 0.13&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0006">6b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0006">6c</a> compares the rate capacities of bare K||bare K and BVO@K||BVO@K, indicating the improved the superb structural stability of the modified K electrode. A smaller voltage hysteresis of 0.18&nbsp;V for BVO@K is displayed in comparison with that of the bare K (0.55&nbsp;V) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0006">6d</a>). The BVO@K exhibits a lower polarization voltage and much less fluctuating compared to the bare K, indicating the BVO protective layer can effectively enhance K ions transport kinetics and realize even K deposition. The chemical components of SEI for BVO@K were investigated by using XPS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25a</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0063_R_d232729959e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Bi 4f spectra present two peaks at 158.5 and 163.6&nbsp;eV, which are related to the Na<sub>3</sub>Bi phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0064_R_d232729959e1268" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - J Energy Chem.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The F 1s spectra present two peaks at 683.0, 684.3, and 687.4&nbsp;eV, which can be assigned to the signals of K–F and S–F groups, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25c</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25d</a> (Supporting Information), the V 2p XPS spectra of the BVO electrode display that two peaks at 516.8 and 524.3&nbsp;eV assigned to the state of V<sup>5+</sup> from K<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> phase. The K–F (592.7&nbsp;eV) and K–O (595.5&nbsp;eV) peaks are observed in the K 2p spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25e</a>, Supporting Information). In the O1s spectra, the four peaks at 532.6 (O─C═O), 531.3 (C─O), and 530.6&nbsp;eV (RO–COOK) are observed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25f</a>, Supporting Information), which originated from the decomposition of the carbonate solvent. The XPS signal of C─C/C═O (284.7&nbsp;eV), C─C (286.3&nbsp;V), and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> (289.5&nbsp;eV) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25g</a> (Supporting Information) confirms the existence of hybrid inorganic and organic compounds, which endows SEI layer with sufficient mechanical strength and inhibit dendrite growth. As for S 2p spectrum, the characteristic signals of KHSO<sub>4</sub> (171.5&nbsp;eV), K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> (169.7&nbsp;eV), and K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> (168.3&nbsp;eV) are observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25h</a> (Supporting Information), which mainly originated from the dissociation of KFSI. These results indicates that the enhanced interfacial stability of BVO@K contains the organic components (e.g., R─C═O) and inorganic components (e.g., K–Bi alloy, KF). Based on the superior cycle stability of BVO@K||BVO@K, a perylene-3,4,9,10-tetracarboxylic dianhydride (PTCDA) was used as cathode (BVO@K||PTCDA) to evaluate the practical energy application of the full cell (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202307628-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202307628-bib-0065_R_d232729959e1319" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Mater.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0006">6e</a>,&nbsp;BVO@K||PTCDA shows reversible capacities of 135, 131, 125, 109, and 58 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1–2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and still maintains a high capacity of 132 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current come back to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As a comparison, the bare K||PTCDA displays a capacity of 36 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Compared to bare K||PTCDA, the smaller polarization voltage of BVO@K||PTCDA is observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0006">6f,g</a>), confirming improved electrochemical reaction kinetics, and realizing a superior rate capability. The long cycle performance of BVO@K||PTCDA is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202307628-fig-0006">6h</a>. The value of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for BVO@K||PTCDA decreases after cycles and subsequent remains relatively stable after 50th cycle in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information), indicating the excellent interfacial stability of BVO@K||PTCDA. By contrast, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of bare K||PTCDA increases gradually from 390 Ω at 50th cycle to 510 Ω at 100th cycle. The BVO@K||PTCDA still delivers a capacity of 72 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high CE of 99.8% at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 740 cycles. By contrast, bare K||PTCDA displays a low capacity of 77 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 235 cycles. The BVO@K||PTCDA displays much lower voltage hysteresis (0.53&nbsp;V) in comparison with that of the bare K||PTCDA (0.83&nbsp;V) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics and superior stability of BVO@K||PTCDA.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202307628-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cf04633e-cc4d-4a17-9679-6ec4801254ff/adfm202307628-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cf04633e-cc4d-4a17-9679-6ec4801254ff/adfm202307628-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4ac2ea88-dddc-4988-b48d-4aaa3d01796e/adfm202307628-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cf04633e-cc4d-4a17-9679-6ec4801254ff/adfm202307628-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202307628-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202307628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cycling performance of symmetrical bare K and BVO@K cells at a) 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 0.5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and b) 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 2.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. c) Rate performance. d) Plating/stripping profiles of symmetrical bare K and BVO@K cells at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. e) Rate performance of bare K||PTCDA and BVO@K||PTCDA. Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles for both f) BVO@K||PTCDA and g) bare K||PTCDA obtained at 0.1–2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. h) Cycling stability of BVO@K||PTCDA at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202307628-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202307628-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a multifunctional heterogeneous interfacial layer has been constructed on the Na (K) metal surface via a spontaneous reaction for Na (K) dendrite-free anode. The designed multifunctional heterogeneous interphase layer not only reduces the Na<sup>+</sup> (K<sup>+</sup>) diffusion barrier, as well as block electron transport across the heterogeneous interfacial layer, but also provides enough sodiophilic (potassiophilic) sites with the help of Na<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> (K<sub>3</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>), achieving to regulate the Na (K) deposition behavior. In addition, the high mechanical toughness of the multifunctional protective layer can effectively inhibit the formation of Na (K) dendrite. Therefore, BVO@Na can operate for over 960&nbsp;h at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> with a low voltage polarization (0.09&nbsp;V). When paired with the NVP cathode, BVO@Na||NVP full cell displays remarkable cyclic stability (over 1900 cycles) and superior rate capability (91 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>). As for BVO@K||BVO@K, it also can operate stably for 870&nbsp;h at 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 2.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The BVO@K||PTCDA delivers excellent cyclic stability and an average Coulombic efficiency can reach up to 99.8% at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Thus, our work may provide an effective interfacial modification strategy to realize a dendrite-free Na (K) anode for the high performance of alkali metal-based batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
